Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conditions for the Judge to Sell Movable Property of an Absent Person
Branches on the judge's authority over absent persons' property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge may sell the movable property of an absent or missing person and apply them to a scenario involving fear of loss.

Core Idea
A judge may sell an absent person's movables only if destruction is feared and the owner's location is unknown.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on the judge's authority to manage the property of those who are absent (ghā’ib) or missing (mafqūd). The rulings balance the need to preserve property from loss while respecting the owner's rights. We will focus on when the judge may sell movable property, using the example of a usurped slave girl to see how the conditions operate in practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between absent and missing persons
Identify the two categories: ghā’ib (location known) and mafqūd (missing, without news). The judge's authority differs, and the condition for sale depends on this distinction.
The text uses 'غائب' and 'مفقود' with distinct rulings.
2
Identify the condition for sale
The judge may sell movables only if there is fear of destruction (khawf al-talaf) and the location of the owner is unknown. If the location is known, the judge should contact the owner instead.
3
Apply to the slave girl case
Work through the fatwa of Najm al-Dīn: a usurped slave girl is in danger of being lost and causing fitna; the judge sells her to avoid loss, preserving the owner's right to the price from the current holder.
4
Note other actions allowed and prohibited
The judge may deposit, lend, sell, and make kitaba, but cannot marry off a slave girl of an absent or mad person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع متعلقة بالتصرف في مال الغائب منقولة من جامع الفصولين: للقاضي ولاية إيداع مال غائب ومفقود وله إقراضه وبيع منقوله لو خيف تلفه ولم يعلم مكان الغائب لا لو علم إذ يمكنه البعث إليه إذا خاف التلف فيمكنه حفظ العين والمالية جميعاً، ولا بيع القاضي الأمة المغصوبة إذا غاب مالكها إنما يبيع مال المفقود. سئل نجم الدين عن أمة أمير وهب خادمه فأخبرته أنها التاجر قتل في فأخذت وتداولتها الأيدي حتى وقعت بيد هذا الأمير والموهوب له الآن لا يجد ورثة القتيل ويعلم أنه لو خلاها ضاعت وإن أمسكها يخاف الفتنة، هل للقاضي بيعها من ذي اليد نيابة عن الغائب حتى لو ظهر المالك كان له على ذي اليد ثمنها؟ قال : نعم له ذلك القاضي لا يملك تزويج أمة الغائب والمجنون وقنهما وله أن يكاتبهما ويبيعهما. لا يملك تزويج أمة الغائب وإن لم يكن له مال للقاضي بيع من المفقود وأمته لا لو كان غائباً غير مفقود وللقاضي ولاية بيع مال الغائب مات ولا يعلم له وارث وكذا قولهما في الظاهر وعنهما أن له بيعه كعروضه وعلى هذا الخلاف بيع عروضه ونفقة امرأته، وفي العقار عنهما روايتان. ثم ذكر المسألة الأخيرة الآتية في الفروع ثم قال: له بيع منقول المفقود ولا ينبغي له أن يبيع عقاره ولو باع جاز. فروع متعلقة بالتصرف في مال الغائب قوله : (لو خيف تلفه ولم يعلم مكان الغائب) قال في النهر : والذي ينبغي أن يقال إن خوف التلف مجوز للبيع علم مكانه أو لا، وقدمنا نحوه في خيار الشرط فارجع إليه اهـ. وفي الولوالجية: رجل اشترى لحماً أو سمكاً فذهب ليجيء بالثمن فأبطأ فخاف البائع أن يفسد يسع للبائع أن يبيعه من غيره ويسع للمشتري أن يشتريه وإن علم بالقضية، أما
Branches related to disposition of the property of an absent person, transmitted from Jami' al-Fusulayn: The judge has authority to deposit the property of an absent or missing person, and to lend it out, and to sell his movable property if its destruction is feared and the location of the absent person is not known; not if it is known, because then he can send a message to him, and if he fears destruction, he can preserve both the property and its value. And the judge does not sell a slave girl who has been usurped if her owner is absent; he only sells the property of a missing person. Najm al-Din was asked about a slave girl that an amir gave to his servant, who told him that the merchant had been killed, so she was taken and passed through many hands until she ended up with this amir and the donee now cannot find the heirs of the killed person, and knows that if he leaves her she will be lost, and if he keeps her he fears tribulation. Is it permissible for the judge to sell her from the current possessor on behalf of the absent owner so that if the owner appears, he can claim the price from the possessor? He said: Yes, he has that. The judge does not have authority to marry off a slave girl of an absent person or a mad person, nor their slave, but he may make a kitaba contract with them and sell them. He does not have authority to marry off a slave girl of an absent person. If the absent person has no money, the judge may sell the property of a missing person and his slave girl, but not if he is merely absent and not missing. The judge has authority to sell the property of an absent person who has died and it is not known whether he has an heir. Likewise, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in the apparent view, and from them that he may sell it like his movables. On this dispute is the sale of his movables and maintenance of his wife. Regarding real estate, there are two narrations from them. Then he mentioned the last case coming in the branches and then said: He may sell the movable property of a missing person, but he should not sell his real estate; and if he sells, it is valid. Branches related to disposition of the property of an absent person. He said: (If its destruction is feared and the location of the absent person is not known) The author of al-Nahr said: What should be said is that fear of destruction permits sale whether his location is known or not. And we have presented similar in the option of condition, so refer to it. End quote. And in al-Walwaljiya: A man bought meat or fish and went to bring the price but was delayed, and the seller feared it would spoil; it is permitted for the seller to sell it to someone else, and it is permitted for the buyer to buy it even if he knows the matter. However,
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a collection of branches (furu') from Jami' al-Fusulayn regarding the judge's authority over the property of an absent (gha'ib) or missing (mafqud) person. The judge may deposit the property, lend it out, and sell movable property if there is fear of destruction and the absent person's location is unknown. If the location is known, the judge should communicate rather than sell. The passage distinguishes between a missing person (whose whereabouts are entirely unknown) and an absent person (whose location is known), with different rulings. For example, the judge can sell the movable property of a missing person but not of a merely absent person unless destruction is feared. There is also a specific case about a usurped slave girl: the judge may sell her on behalf of the absent owner to avoid loss and fitna. The judge cannot marry off a slave girl of an absent or mad person. The text also discusses the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding selling real estate of an absent person, with two narrations. Finally, it references that fear of destruction permits sale regardless of knowing the location, citing al-Nahr and al-Walwaljiya. This passage illustrates the nuanced Hanafi jurisprudence on protecting property rights when the owner is not present.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqūd): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and no news is received; a missing person whose life or death is uncertain.
ولاية (wilāyah): The authority of the judge to manage the property of an absent or missing person, including custody, lending, and sale.
خوف التلف (khawf al-talaf): Fear that the property will be destroyed or perish if not sold immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the judge to sell the movable property of an absent person?
A) The absent person must have died.
B) The property must be real estate.
C) There must be fear of destruction and the location of the absent person must be unknown.
D) The judge must obtain permission from the absent person's relatives.

Key Takeaway
The judge's authority to sell an absent person's movable property is conditional on fear of destruction and unknown location or status as missing.

The Dispute on Selling Real Estate of a Missing Person and the Al-Walwaljiya Case
Branches on the judge's authority, including real estate dispute and perishable goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the judge may sell real estate of a missing person and apply the ruling that sale is valid but not recommended, and also identify the permissibility of selling perishable goods under fear of spoilage regardless of the owner's knowledge.

Core Idea
The judge may sell a missing person's real estate, but it is not recommended; for perishable goods, fear of spoilage alone permits sale.

This lesson continues the discussion of the judge's authority over the property of absent and missing persons, focusing on two distinct issues: the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of real estate, and a practical example from al-Walwaljiya about selling perishable goods when the buyer delays payment. The former shows that even within the Hanafi school there is disagreement on the scope of the judge's authority, while the latter illustrates that fear of spoilage can override the general condition of unknown location.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute on real estate
Identify the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Hanifa, the judge should not sell real estate of a missing person, but if he does, the sale is valid. The Sahibayn allow the sale altogether.
The phrase 'ولا ينبغي له أن يبيع عقاره ولو باع جاز' captures Abu Hanifa's view.
2
Examine the two narrations from the Sahibayn
The text mentions that there are two narrations from the Sahibayn regarding real estate: one permitting sale and one not. This indicates some internal difference even among the students of Abu Hanifa.
3
Apply the rule to movables and maintenance
The dispute on real estate is analogously applied to the sale of movables and maintenance of the wife of an absent person, as stated in the text.
4
Analyze the al-Walwaljiya example
In a case where a buyer delays payment for meat or fish, the seller fears spoilage and sells to another. The ruling permits the original seller to sell, and the first buyer may also buy it again even if he knows the situation.
This shows that fear of destruction is a strong basis for sale, even when the counterparty is aware.
5
Reconcile with the earlier condition
The author of al-Nahr used this example to argue that fear of destruction permits sale regardless of whether the owner's location is known, but the original text restricts the condition to unknown location for absent persons.

Source Text

Protecting the Absent Debtor's Property: The Qadi's Duty
Ruling on distribution of absent debtor's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the qadi's handling of property belonging to an absent debtor when two persons acknowledge a claim, and apply it to a new scenario.

Core Idea
The qadi must not distribute an absent debtor's property until the debtor or his heirs appear.

In Hanafi fiqh, when two persons acknowledge a debt against an absent person, and there is property belonging to that absent person in the city, the qadi is prohibited from handing over that property to the claimants. This ruling safeguards the absent party's rights until they or their legal representatives can attend. Let's explore the exact condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The qadi does not pay anything from the property until the heirs (if the debtor is deceased) or the debtor himself (if alive) attend.
This is the core prohibition.
2
Understand the condition
The condition for distribution is the presence of either the heirs or the debtor. The qadi must wait for either, not both.
The text uses 'or' (حتى تحضر ورثته أو يحضر المقضى عليه).
3
Recognize the rationale
The purpose is to protect the absent party's right to defend themselves or for their estate to be properly represented.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on procedural fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومال في المصر عند المقرين به للمقضى عليه فالقاضي لا يدفع شيئاً منه حتى تحضر ورثته أو يحضر المقضى عليه لو غائباً
And property in the city, in the presence of two acknowledgers, belonging to the person against whom the judgment is passed, the qadi does not pay anything from it until his heirs attend or the judgment debtor attends if he is absent.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, commented upon in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It addresses a scenario where two persons acknowledge a debt or claim against an absent person, and there is property in the city. The qadi is not to hand over that property to the claimants until the absent person's heirs (if deceased) or the person himself appears. This ensures protection of the absent party's rights. The reference 'kadha fi Jami' al-Fusulayn' confirms it is established in that source.

Key Terms
المقرين (al-muqirrayn): Two persons who acknowledge (a debt or claim) against the absent person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met before the qadi distributes property acknowledged by two persons against an absent debtor?
A) Both heirs and debtor must be present.
B) The debtor must have died.
C) Either the heirs or the debtor must attend.
D) The qadi must have written permission.

Key Takeaway
The qadi must never distribute an absent person's property until the person or their heirs are present.

Qadi's Authority in Hire and Pawn Cases
Two cases illustrating qadi's authority to sell property of absent owners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the qadi's authority to sell property of absent parties in hire and pawn cases and apply the same principle to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
When a contract dissolves or the owner vanishes, the qadi may sell the property to restore fairness.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two practical cases where the qadi acts as a guardian over the property of those who are deceased or absent without trace. In both cases, the qadi's intervention is permissible (jā'iz) and serves to settle debts and prevent loss. Understanding these rulings helps you grasp the scope of qadi's authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The first case involves hired camels where the owner dies en route, dissolving the contract. The second involves a pawned item where the debtor disappears.
2
State the common element
In both, the owner is absent or dead, and the other party has a financial claim. The qadi intervenes as a guardian.
3
Extract the ruling
The qadi may sell the property (camel or pawn) to satisfy the claim, refunding any excess to the absent party or hirer.
4
Note the conditions
For the hire case: death of owner, dissolution of contract, and qadi's discretion. For the pawn case: the absence must be ghaybah munqati'ah (prolonged and unknown location).
5
Reference the source
Both cases are taken from Jami' al-Fusulayn, a respected Hanafi text, indicating the authority of this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف رحمه الله تعالى إلى أن من استأجر إبلاً إلى مكة ذاهباً وجائياً ودفع الكراء ومات رب الدابة في الذهاب حتى انفسخت الإجارة فإذا أتى مكة ورفع الأمر إلى القاضي فرأى أن يبيع الدابة ويدفع بعض الأجر إلى المستأجر ،جاز، وللمستأجر أن يركبها إلى مكة ولا يضمن وعليه الكراء إلى مكة، وإلى أن المديون لو رهن وغاب غيبة منقطعة فرفع المرتهن الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يبيع الرهن بدينه فإنه ينبغي أن يجوز كما في هاتين المسألتين والمسألتان في جامع الفصولين.
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated that whoever hires camels to Mecca going and returning, and paid the hire, and the owner of the camel dies on the outgoing journey, resulting in the dissolution of the hire contract, then when he comes to Mecca and refers the matter to the qadi, and the qadi sees fit to sell the camel and pay some of the hire to the hirer, it is permissible. And the hirer may ride it to Mecca without liability, and he owes the hire to Mecca. And that if a debtor pawns and goes on an extended absence, and the pawn-holder refers the matter to the qadi to sell the pawn for his debt, then it should be permissible, as in these two cases. And the two cases are in Jami' al-Fusulayn.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof by reference to Jami' al-Fusulayn. It provides two examples of the qadi's guardianship over absent persons' property. In the first, a hirer of camels paid the hire in advance; the camel owner dies en route, dissolving the contract. The qadi may sell the camel and refund part of the hire to the hirer, and the hirer may continue riding to Mecca without liability for the camel, but still owes the hire for the portion used. In the second, a debtor pawns an item and then disappears; the pawn-holder may request the qadi to sell the pawn to recover the debt. Both cases establish the qadi's authority to act in the best interest of the parties involved.

Key Terms
غيبة منقطعة (ghaybah munqati'ah): An absence that is prolonged and cut off, where the person's location is unknown
انفسخت الإجارة (infasakhat al-ijarah): Dissolution of the hire contract due to the death of the lessor

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of the camel owner's death on the outward journey?
A) The hire contract becomes void and the hirer must return immediately.
B) The hire contract dissolves, and the qadi may sell the camel and refund part of the hire.
C) The hirer inherits the camel.
D) The hirer must pay full hire and find another camel.

Key Takeaway
The qadi has the authority to sell the property of deceased or absent parties to settle financial claims in hire and pawn cases.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Qadi's Permission to Sell Undelivered Beast
Qadi's authority to authorize seller to resell undelivered beast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the qadi's supervisory role over an undelivered sold beast and identify the legal consequences of the seller acting without the qadi's permission.

Core Idea
Without qadi's authorization, a seller's resale of an undelivered beast is a suspended transaction (fuduli) and delivery makes him a usurper.

When a seller has sold a beast but not delivered it to the buyer, the property is in a state of limbo. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the qadi may step in to authorize the seller to sell, lease, or maintain the beast. Without such authorization, the seller's actions risk being legally defective and may create liability for both seller and buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The seller has sold a beast but has not yet delivered it to the buyer. The beast is still in the seller's possession but belongs to the buyer in principle. The buyer is absent or unresponsive.
This is a common situation in sales where possession has not been transferred.
2
Define the qadi's options
The qadi (ruler or judge) may authorize the seller to resell the beast, to lease it, or to feed it from the lease income. The qadi can also appoint an agent to sell it.
The qadi acts as a representative of the public interest and the absent buyer's rights.
3
Analyze the unauthorized action
If the seller resells without the qadi's permission, the new sale is a fuduli contract — suspended and not binding until ratified. If the seller delivers the beast to the new buyer, the seller becomes a transgressor (muta'addi) and the new buyer becomes a usurper (ghasib), liable for the full value.
Fuduli and ghasib are key Hanafi concepts for unauthorized transactions.
4
Apply the principle
The underlying principle is that the seller cannot unilaterally dispose of property that no longer belongs to him. The qadi's permission substitutes for the absent buyer's consent and ensures fairness.
This protects the interests of both seller and buyer until delivery is completed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً: باع دابة ولم يوقف على المشتري فللحاكم أن يأذن له في بيعها فيأخذ ثمنه من ثمنه لو كان من جنسه ولو أذن له أن يؤجرها ويعلفها من أجرها جاز ا.هـ. وبه علم أن في مسألة الكتاب للقاضي أن يأذن للبائع في بيعها كما له أن يبيعها بنفسه أو أمينه، وأن له أن يأذن له في إجارتها لو كان لها أجر وظاهر كلامهم أن البائع لا يملك البيع بلا إذن القاضي فإن باع كان فضولياً، وإن سلم كان متعدياً والمشتري منه غاضب.
And in it also: He sold a beast and did not deliver it to the buyer, so the ruler may authorize him to sell it, and he takes his price from its price if it is of the same type. And if he authorizes him to lease it and feed it from its hire, it is permissible. End quote. From this it is known that in the matter of the book, the qadi may authorize the seller to sell it, just as he may sell it himself or through his trustee, and that he may authorize him to lease it if it has hire. And the apparent meaning of their words is that the seller does not have the right to sell without the qadi's permission, for if he sells, it is a contract of a fiduciary (fuduli), and if he delivers, he is a transgressor and the buyer from him is a usurper (ghasib).
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary. It deals with a scenario where a seller has sold a beast but not delivered it to the buyer. The qadi may authorize the seller to resell it, and the seller takes his own price (if similar in type) from the new sale. The qadi may also allow the seller to lease the beast and use its hire for feeding. The seller cannot act independently; without qadi's permission, a sale is fuduli (not binding unless ratified) and delivery would make the seller a transgressor and the buyer a usurper. This highlights the qadi's supervisory role over property where parties are absent.

Key Terms
فضولياً (fuduliyan): A contract made by a person without authority, not binding until ratified by the owner
غاضب (ghasib): A usurper, one who takes property without right

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The sale is valid and the seller keeps the profit
B) The sale is fuduli (suspended) and the seller may be a transgressor
C) The sale is automatically null and void
D) The seller is not liable because the buyer delayed

Key Takeaway
The qadi's permission is a necessary condition for the seller to validly dispose of an undelivered sold beast; without it, the transaction is suspended and the seller risks liability.

Qadi's Authority over Absent and Missing Persons' Property
Branches on qadi's disposal of absent persons' property with an internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the limits of the qadi's authority over the property of absent and missing persons, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of real estate of a deceased absent person.

Core Idea
The qadi can sell movables of a missing person but should avoid selling real estate unless necessary.

The qadi (judge) has guardianship over the property of those who cannot manage it themselves, including absent persons (gha'ib) and missing persons (mafqud). However, this authority is not unlimited. This lesson examines the conditions under which the qadi may dispose of such property, focusing on an internal Hanafi dispute about selling real estate when the absent owner dies with no heir. The relied-upon position distinguishes between movables and immovables, a key difference for legal practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between gha'ib and mafqud
Identify the two categories: gha'ib (absent but location known) and mafqud (missing, unknown location). The qadi's authority differs: for gha'ib, he can only act if location unknown and property at risk; for mafqud, broader authority exists.
2
State the general rules of disposal
List what the qadi can do: deposit, loan, sell movables if risk of loss and location unknown (for gha'ib) or freely (for mafqud). Note that he cannot sell a usurped slave-girl of an absent owner unless through special circumstances.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
When an absent person dies with no heir, can the qadi sell his real estate? Abu Hanifa and the apparent view of the Sahibayn say he may sell all property like movables. But there is also a narration from the Sahibayn restricting it, and two narrations on real estate.
The dispute is about the nature of the qadi's authority over real property of the deceased absent.
4
Apply the tarjih (relied-upon view)
The text concludes: the qadi may sell movables of the missing person, but should not sell real estate. If he does sell, the sale is valid but blameworthy. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The tarjih balances flexibility with caution, preserving the property interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير من فروع متعلقة بالتصرف في مال الغائب منقولة من جامع الفصولين: للقاضي ولاية إيداع مال غائب ومفقود وله إقراضه وبيع منقوله لو خيف تلفه ولم يعلم مكان الغائب لا لو علم إذ يمكنه البعث إليه إذا خاف التلف فيمكنه حفظ العين والمالية جميعاً، ولا بيع القاضي الأمة المغصوبة إذا غاب مالكها إنما يبيع مال المفقود. سئل نجم الدين عن أمة أمير وهب خادمه فأخبرته أنها التاجر قتل في فأخذت وتداولتها الأيدي حتى وقعت بيد هذا الأمير والموهوب له الآن لا يجد ورثة القتيل ويعلم أنه لو خلاها ضاعت وإن أمسكها يخاف الفتنة، هل للقاضي بيعها من ذي اليد نيابة عن الغائب حتى لو ظهر المالك كان له على ذي اليد ثمنها؟ قال : نعم له ذلك القاضي لا يملك تزويج أمة الغائب والمجنون وقنهما وله أن يكاتبهما ويبيعهما. لا يملك تزويج أمة الغائب وإن لم يكن له مال للقاضي بيع من المفقود وأمته لا لو كان غائباً غير مفقود وللقاضي ولاية بيع مال الغائب مات ولا يعلم له وارث وكذا قولهما في الظاهر وعنهما أن له بيعه كعروضه وعلى هذا الخلاف بيع عروضه ونفقة امرأته، وفي العقار عنهما روايتان. ثم ذكر المسألة الأخيرة الآتية في الفروع ثم قال: له بيع منقول المفقود ولا ينبغي له أن يبيع عقاره ولو باع جاز.
Other branches related to disposal of the absent person's property, transmitted from Jami' al-Fusulayn: The qadi has guardianship to deposit the property of an absent or missing person, and to lend it, and to sell his movable property if its destruction is feared and the place of the absent person is not known, but not if it is known, because then he can send to him when he fears destruction, so he can preserve both the physical object and the value. And the qadi does not sell a usurped slave-girl if her owner is absent; rather, he sells the property of the missing person. Najm al-Din was asked about a slave-girl of an amir whom he gifted to his servant, and she informed him that it was the property of a merchant who was killed, and it was taken and passed through hands until it fell into the hands of this amir, and the donee now cannot find the heirs of the slain man, and knows that if he leaves her she will be lost, and if he keeps her he fears trouble. Is it permissible for the qadi to sell her from the possessor on behalf of the absent person, so that if the owner appears, he has a claim against the possessor for her price? He said: Yes, that is permissible for him. The qadi does not have authority to marry off the slave-girl of an absent or insane person, nor their male slave, but he may enter into a kitaba contract with them and sell them. He does not have authority to marry off a slave-girl of an absent person. And if the absent person has no property, the qadi may sell the property of a missing person, including his slave-girl. But not if he is merely absent (not missing). And the qadi has authority to sell the property of an absent person who died and no heir is known. Similarly, the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in the apparent position, and from both of them that he may sell it like his other movable goods. And on this difference is the sale of his movable goods and the maintenance of his wife. And regarding real estate, there are two narrations from them. Then he mentioned the last case that follows in the branches, then said: He may sell the movable property of the missing person, but he should not sell his real estate, and if he sells, it is valid.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from Jami' al-Fusulayn on the qadi's authority over the property of absent and missing persons. It begins with general rules: the qadi may deposit or loan the property, and sell movables only if destruction is feared and the place of the absent person is unknown; if known, the qadi can contact him. A specific case is discussed regarding a usurped slave-girl that has passed through multiple hands; Najm al-Din rules that the qadi may sell her from the current possessor on behalf of the absent original owner. The qadi has no authority to marry off slaves of an absent or insane person, but may execute kitaba or sell them. If the absent person has no property, the qadi sells property of a missing person, but not of a merely absent person. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute appears: when an absent person dies with no known heir, the qadi has authority to sell his property. Abu Hanifa's position (with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the apparent view) is that he may sell it like other movables. There is also a difference regarding the sale of his movable goods and maintenance of his wife. For real estate, there are two narrations. The relied-upon view is that he may sell movables of the missing person but should not sell real estate; however, if he does sell, the sale is valid.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-gha'ib): A person who is absent but whose location is known
المفقود (al-mafqud): A missing person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life status is uncertain
المكاتبة (al-mukatabah): A contract of manumission by installments (kitaba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He may sell it only if destruction is feared
B) He may sell it freely
C) He may sell it but not real estate
D) He may not sell it at all

Key Takeaway
The qadi's authority over a missing person's property allows sale of movables, but real estate should be preserved unless necessary; even then, the sale is valid.

Refining the Condition for Selling Absentee Property
Refining condition that place must be unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which the qadi may sell an absent person's movable property according to al-Nahr, and apply the same principle to perishable goods sales.

Core Idea
Fear of destruction, not unknown location, permits sale of absent person's property.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author refines a condition previously stated in the matn. The original text required both fear of destruction and unknown location of the absent owner for the qadi to sell. The commentator al-Nahr argues that fear of destruction alone is sufficient, because the purpose is to prevent loss. He supports this by referring to an earlier discussion on khiyar al-shart. Then a case from al-Walwaljiyya illustrates a similar ruling for perishable goods when the buyer delays payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original condition
The original matn required two conditions for the qadi to sell an absent person's movable property: (1) fear of destruction, and (2) the place of the absent person is unknown. Understand these from the base text.
This is the ruling being refined.
2
Understand al-Nahr's critique
Al-Nahr argues that the condition of unknown location is unnecessary. Fear of destruction alone justifies sale because the goal is preservation of value. He states: 'Fear of destruction permits sale whether his place is known or not.'
This is a critical refinement based on the purpose (maqsad).
3
Examine the analogy to perishable goods
A similar case: a buyer delays payment for meat or fish, and the seller fears spoilage. The seller may resell to another, and the original buyer may also buy it back. The effective cause (illa) is preventing loss of property.
This illustrates the principle beyond the qadi's sale.
4
Apply the refined rule to a new scenario
Practice: If a man's fruit crop is rotting and he is in a known location, the qadi may still sell it because fear of destruction exists. The location condition is dropped.
Consistent with al-Nahr's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع متعلقة بالتصرف في مال الغائب قوله : (لو خيف تلفه ولم يعلم مكان الغائب) قال في النهر : والذي ينبغي أن يقال إن خوف التلف مجوز للبيع علم مكانه أو لا، وقدمنا نحوه في خيار الشرط فارجع إليه اهـ. وفي الولوالجية: رجل اشترى لحماً أو سمكاً فذهب ليجيء بالثمن فأبطأ فخاف البائع أن يفسد يسع للبائع أن يبيعه من غيره ويسع للمشتري أن يشتريه وإن علم بالقضية.
Branches related to disposal of the absent person's property: His saying: (If its destruction is feared and the place of the absent person is not known). He said in al-Nahr: What should be said is that fear of destruction permits sale whether his place is known or not, and we have preceded similar in the chapter on option of condition, so refer to it. End. And in al-Walwaljiyya: A man bought meat or fish, then went to bring the price and delayed, and the seller feared it would spoil; it is permissible for the seller to sell it to someone else, and it is permissible for the buyer to buy it even if he knows about the situation.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the condition mentioned in the previous ruling. The original text restricted the qadi's sale of an absent person's movable property to cases where both destruction is feared and the place of the absent person is unknown. Al-Nahr challenges this, stating that fear of destruction alone is sufficient to permit sale, whether the place is known or not, because the goal is preservation of value. He references earlier discussion in the chapter on khiyar al-shart. Then a case from al-Walwaljiyya illustrates a similar principle: if a buyer delays paying for perishable goods (meat or fish), the seller may resell them to avoid spoilage, and the original buyer may also buy them back even knowing the situation. This is based on istihsan (equitable preference) to prevent loss.

Key Terms
خوف التلف (khawf al-talaf): Fear of physical spoilage or destruction of property
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of condition in a sale contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only if destruction is feared
C) Only if location is unknown
D) At any time

Key Takeaway
Al-Nahr's refinement: fear of destruction alone permits sale of absent person's property, and the same principle applies to perishable goods.

Half Gold, Half Silver: Interpreting Mixed Price Terms
Interpreting ambiguous price term: half gold, half silver mithqals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the correct interpretation of two ambiguous price formulations: 'X mithqals of gold and silver' and 'X from gold and silver', and APPLY the Hanafi rules for each.

Core Idea
The exact wording of a price term determines whether each metal is paid by weight or by coin count.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates how the Hanafi madhhab resolves ambiguity in sale contracts when the price is stated in two types of precious metals. The difference between saying 'one thousand mithqals of gold and silver' and 'one thousand from gold and silver' leads to different payment obligations. Understanding this linguistic precision protects against contractual disputes and ensures valid sales.

Guided Path
1
Parse the first formulation
When the price is 'one thousand mithqals of gold and silver', the word 'mithqal' is attributed equally to both metals. Therefore, the total is split equally: 500 mithqals of gold and 500 mithqals of silver.
This is because there is no priority (awlawiyya) for one over the other.
2
Identify the quality condition
The text states that specification of quality (good or bad) is required. If not specified, the default is good quality silver (and presumably gold).
Compared to when the price is given in dirhams and dinars, where no quality specification is needed.
3
Contrast with the second formulation
If the phrase is 'one thousand from gold and silver' (without 'mithqal' for silver), then the gold portion is still counted in mithqals, but the silver portion is paid in dirhams coins at the customary rate: 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals.
The word 'from' (من) triggers the customary understanding for silver.
4
Understand the reason for the difference
The Hanafi jurists rely on 'urf (custom). For gold, the standard is weight (mithqal); for silver, the standard is coin count (dirhams) unless weight is explicitly mentioned.
This prevents hardship and aligns with market practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن باع أمة بألف مثقال ذهب وفضة فهما نصفان) لأنه أضاف المثقال إليهما على السواء فيجب من كل واحد خمسمائة مثقال لعدم الأولوية فيصير كأنه قال بعت بخمسمائة مثقال ذهب وخمسمائة مثقال فضة. ويشترط بيان من الجودة وغيرها بخلاف ما لو قال من الدراهم والدنانير فإنه لا يحتاج إلى بيان الفضة وينصرف إلى الجياد، وقيد بقوله بألف مثقال لأنه لو باعها بألف من الذهب والفضة فإنه يجب النصف الذهب مثاقيل، ومن الفضة دراهم العشرة منها وزن سبعة مثاقيل لأنه أضاف الألف إليهما فينصرف إلى الوزن المعهود من كل واحد.
His saying: (And whoever sells a slave-girl for one thousand mithqals of gold and silver, they are halves) because he attributed the mithqal to both equally, so from each is due five hundred mithqals due to lack of priority, so it is as if he said: I sold for five hundred mithqals of gold and five hundred mithqals of silver. And specification of quality etc. is required, unlike if he said 'from dirhams and dinars', for then no specification of silver is needed and it is taken to be good quality. And he restricted it by saying 'one thousand mithqals' because if he sold her for 'one thousand from gold and silver', then the half in gold is mithqals, and from silver, dirhams of which ten weigh seven mithqals, because he attributed the thousand to both and so it is taken to the customary weight from each.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling on interpreting ambiguous price terms. When a seller says 'one thousand mithqals of gold and silver', the word mithqal is used for both metals equally, so each half is 500 mithqals. The quality (good or bad) must be specified; if not specified, the default is good quality. However, if the seller said 'one thousand from gold and silver', the interpretation differs: the gold part is mithqals (weight), while the silver part is dirhams, with ten dirhams weighing seven mithqals, according to customary weight. This illustrates the importance of wording in contracts and the Hanafi reliance on customary usage ('urf).

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight, approximately 4.25 grams, used for gold and silver
دراهم (darahim): Silver coins (dirhams), by default of good quality unless specified

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave-girl is sold for 'one thousand mithqals of gold and silver', how much is due from each?
A) 1000 mithqals of gold and 1000 of silver
B) 500 mithqals of gold and 500 of silver
C) 1000 ounces of each
D) The gold is by mithqal and silver by dirham

Key Takeaway
When a sale price is stated as 'X mithqals of gold and silver', the Hanafi rule divides equally between gold and silver mithqals, with quality defaulting to good; if 'from gold and silver' is used, silver is paid in dirhams at the customary rate.

Present Co-Buyer Paying the Absent Partner's Share: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on present co-buyer's right to pay and withhold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding the present co-buyer paying the full price, explain the reasoning (analogy to pledge, upper floor, agent), and apply the ruling to distinguish when the present buyer is a volunteer versus entitled to reimbursement.

Core Idea
The present co-buyer is compelled to pay the full price because the seller may withhold the entire item.

When two people buy an item together and one buyer is absent, the seller has the right to withhold the entire item until the full price is paid. This puts the present buyer in a bind: he must either pay the absent partner's share or lose the item. The Hanafi school debates whether he can pay the full price, take delivery, and then hold the absent partner's share as collateral until reimbursed. This lesson examines the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (majority view) and the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf, along with the analogical reasoning that supports each side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Problem
Recognize the scenario: two co-buyers, one absent. The seller can withhold the entire item until all price is paid. What rights does the present buyer have?
2
State the Majority View (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
The present buyer may pay the full price, take possession of the whole item, and withhold it from the absent partner until reimbursed.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the Analogical Proof (Dalil)
The present buyer is like: (a) the lender of a pledge who must pay to redeem, (b) the owner of an upper floor who must repair the shared building, (c) an agent for purchase who pays from his own money. In each case, one is compelled to spend and can seek reimbursement.
This analogy shows the present buyer is not a volunteer when the partner is absent.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's Objection
Abu Yusuf says the present buyer is a stranger to the absent partner's share; upon payment, he only takes his own share. He cannot act for the absent partner without authorization.
This would cause practical difficulty: the seller would still hold the absent partner's share.
5
Apply the Distinguishing Factor (Presence vs. Absence)
If the absent partner were present, the present buyer paying his share would be a volunteer (no reimbursement). If absent, necessity makes him similar to an agent, so he is not a volunteer.
The same rule does not apply to lease (ijarah) because the lessor has no right to withhold the property for unpaid rent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غاب أحد المشتريين فللحاضر دفع كل الثمن وقبضه وحبسه حتى ينقد شريكه وهذا قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وخالف أبو يوسف في الكل فهذه أحكام : الأول في قبض جميع المبيع على تقدير إيفاء الثمن كله فعنده إذا نقد الثمن لا يأخذ إلا نصيبه لكونه أجنبياً في نصيب الغائب، وهما يقولان : إن الحاضر مضطر إلى أداء كل الثمن لأن للبائع حق حبس كل المبيع إلى أن يستوفي كل الثمن فصار كمعير الرهن وصاحب العلو والوكيل بالشراء إذا أدى الثمن من ماله . قيد بغيبته لأنه لو كان حاضراً لا يقبضه اتفاقاً ويكون متبرعاً لأنه كالوكيل عنه من وجه من حيث إن ملك الغائب ثبت بقبول الحاضر غير وكيل من وجه لأن كلا منهما لا يطالب بنصيب الآخر فلشبهه بالأجنبي كان متبرعاً في حضرته، ولشبهة بالوكيل لم يكن متبرعاً حال غيبته الثاني في حبسه عن الغائب حتى يعطيه ما دفعه عنه وهو فرع أنه ليس بمتبرع عندهما لما قدمناه ودل أن له الرجوع عليه واستفيد من قوله «للحاضر الدفع» أن البائع يجبر على قبول ما أداه الحاضر من نصيب الغائب كما يجبر على تسليم نصيب الغائب فهذه خمسة أحكام على الخلاف وقيد بقوله «أحد المشتريين لأنه لو غاب أحد المستأجرين قبل نقد الأجرة فنقد الحاضر جميعها يكون متبرعاً لأنه غير مضطر في نقد حصة الغائب إذ ليس للآجر حبس الدار لاستيفاء الأجرة
And if one of the two buyers is absent, the present one may pay the entire price and take delivery and withhold it until his partner reimburses him. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf differed in all. So these are rulings: The first regarding taking delivery of all the sold item on the condition of paying the full price. According to him [Abu Yusuf], when he pays the price he only takes his share because he is a stranger in the share of the absent one. And they [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] say: The present one is compelled to pay the entire price because the seller has the right to withhold the entire sold item until he receives the entire price, so it becomes like the lender of a pledge, the owner of the upper floor, and the agent for purchase when he pays the price from his own wealth. It is restricted by his absence because if he were present, he would not take delivery by agreement and he would be a volunteer because he is like an agent for him in one respect, since the ownership of the absent one is established by the acceptance of the present one, but not an agent in another respect because each of them is not demanded for the other's share. So due to his resemblance to a stranger, he is a volunteer in his presence, and due to his resemblance to an agent, he is not a volunteer during his absence. The second: regarding his withholding from the absent one until he gives him what he paid for him, and it is a branch of the fact that he is not a volunteer according to them as we have presented, and it indicates that he has recourse against him. And it is understood from his saying 'the present may pay' that the seller is compelled to accept what the present one paid from the share of the absent one, just as he is compelled to deliver the share of the absent one. So these are five rulings subject to disagreement, and it is restricted by his saying 'one of the two buyers' because if one of the two lessees were absent before paying the rent, and the present one paid all of it, he would be a volunteer because he is not compelled to pay the absent one's share since the lessor does not have the right to withhold the house to collect the rent.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling when one of two co-buyers is absent. The core issue is whether the present buyer can pay the full price, take possession of the entire item, and hold it until the absent partner reimburses him. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, because the seller has a right to withhold the entire item until the full price is paid, so the present buyer is compelled to pay all. Abu Yusuf disagrees: upon payment, the present buyer only takes his share, treating him as a stranger regarding the absent partner's share. The commentary elaborates on the rationale: the present buyer is like an agent for the absent partner in absence but becomes a volunteer if the absent partner is present. It also distinguishes this from the case of a lease (ijarah) where the lessor has no right to withhold the property for unpaid rent, so the present lessee paying the absent's share is a volunteer.

Key Terms
المشتريان (al-mushtarīyān): The two buyers who jointly purchase an item, each owning a share.
الحاضر (al-ḥāḍir): The buyer who is present at the time of transaction, as opposed to the absent partner.
متبرع (mutabarri‘): A volunteer who performs an act without expectation of reimbursement.
مضطر (muḍṭarr): One who is compelled or forced by circumstances to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf's view regarding the present co-buyer?
A) The majority says he can withhold the item; Abu Yusuf says he cannot.
B) The majority says he is a volunteer; Abu Yusuf says he is an agent.
C) The majority says he only takes his share; Abu Yusuf says he takes all.
D) The majority says the seller must accept the full payment; Abu Yusuf says the seller can refuse.

Key Takeaway
The present co-buyer, when the partner is absent, is compelled to pay the full price due to the seller's right to withhold, and may hold the item as collateral—this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Selling for a Thousand Mithqals of Gold and Silver
Ruling on interpretation of ambiguous currency units in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret ambiguous price specifications in sales contracts and apply the Hanafi rule of equal division when a unit is shared between two metals.

Core Idea
When a price is expressed as 'X mithqals of gold and silver', it is split equally because the unit is applied to both without priority.

When a sales contract mentions a price in mixed terms like 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver,' how should the amount be divided? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi rule: because the word 'mithqal' is added equally to both metals, the obligation is half gold and half silver. The ruling also shows how custom ('urf) determines the meaning of 'dirham' in different lands, which affects transactions like sales, dower, and waqf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
The price phrase 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver' contains an ambiguity: is it 1000 of each or 500 of each? The grammar attaches 'mithqal' equally to both metals.
2
Apply the linguistic rule
Since 'mithqal' is shared equally between 'gold' and 'silver' via the conjunction 'and', the default interpretation is equal division (500 each) unless custom indicates otherwise.
This follows the principle that when a term applies to multiple items equally, priority is not given to one without evidence.
3
Consider the role of custom for dirhams
If the phrase is 'a thousand from gold and silver' (without mithqal), the silver portion is in dirhams, not mithqals. The weight of a dirham varies by local custom; the Hanafi school relies on the custom of the land of the contract.
Example: In Syria and Hijaz, a dirham may be lighter; in Egypt, 'dirham' defaults to copper fulus unless specified as silver or 'nuqra'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن باع أمة بألف مثقال ذهب وفضة فهما نصفان) لأنه أضاف المثقال إليهما على السواء فيجب من كل واحد خمسمائة مثقال لعدم الأولوية فيصير كأنه قال بعت بخمسمائة مثقال ذهب وخمسمائة مثقال فضة. ويشترط بيان من الجودة وغيرها بخلاف ما لو قال من الدراهم والدنانير فإنه لا يحتاج إلى بيان الفضة وينصرف إلى الجياد، وقيد بقوله بألف مثقال لأنه لو باعها بألف من الذهب والفضة فإنه يجب النصف الذهب مثاقيل، ومن الفضة دراهم العشرة منها وزن سبعة مثاقيل لأنه أضاف الألف إليهما فينصرف إلى الوزن المعهود من كل واحد. وأشار المؤلف إلى أنه لو قال لفلان علي كر حنطة وشعير وسمسم فإنه يجب من كل جنس ثلث الكر، وهكذا في المعاملات كلها كالمهر والوصية والوديعة والغصب والإجارة وبدل الخلع وغيره في الموزون والمكيل والمعدود والمذروع. وفي فتح القدير في الدراهم ينصرف إلى الوزن المعهود وزن سبعة، ويجب كون هذا إذا كان المتعارف في بلد العقد في اسم الدراهم ما يوزن سبعة. والمتعارف في بعض البلاد الآن كالشام والحجاز ليس ذلك بل وزن ربع وقيراط الدرهم . وأما في عرف مصر لفظ الدرهم ينصرف الآن إلى زنة أربعة دراهم بوزن سبعة من الفلوس إلا أن يقيد بالفضة فينصرف إلى درهم بوزن سبعة فإن ما دونه ثقل أو خفة يسمونه نصف فضة اهـ . وعلى هذا إذا شرط بعض الواقفين بمصر للمستحق دراهم ولم يقيدها تنصرف إلى الفلوس النحاس، وأما إذا قيدها بالنقرة كواقف الشيخونية والصرغتمشية تنصرف إلى الفضة لما في المغرب : النقرة القطعة المذابة من الذهب أو الفضة، ويقال نقرة فضة على الإضافة للبيان ا هـ. وفي المصباح : النقرة القطعة المذابة من الفضة وقبل الذوب هي تبر ا هـ .
His saying: (And whoever sells a slave-girl for a thousand mithqals of gold and silver, they are equal halves) because he added the mithqal to both equally, so from each one must be five hundred mithqal due to lack of priority, so it is as if he said: I sold for five hundred mithqals of gold and five hundred mithqals of silver. And specification of quality and others is required, contrary to if he said from dirhams and dinars, for then specification of silver is not needed and it is interpreted as the best. And it is restricted by his saying 'a thousand mithqal' because if he sold her for a thousand from gold and silver, then half from gold is in mithqals, and from silver, dirhams, ten of which weigh seven mithqals, because he added the thousand to both, so it is interpreted according to the customary weight from each one. And the author indicated that if he said, 'I owe a kor of wheat, barley, and sesame,' then from each type one-third of a kor is due, and likewise in all transactions like dower, bequest, deposit, usurpation, lease, khul' compensation, etc., in weighed, measured, counted, and measured-by-length items. And in Fath al-Qadir, dirhams are interpreted as the customary weight of seven [mithqals], and this is when the custom in the land of the contract for the term dirham is what weighs seven. And the custom in some lands now like Syria and Hijaz is not that, but rather the weight is a quarter and a qirat of a dirham. As for the custom in Egypt, the term dirham now refers to the weight of four dirhams of weight seven from copper coins (fulus), unless it is restricted by silver, then it refers to a dirham of weight seven; indeed, whatever is below that in weight or lightness they call half-silver. So on this basis, if some endower (waqif) in Egypt specifies dirhams for the beneficiaries and does not restrict them, it refers to copper fulus. But if he restricts them with 'nuqra' like the endowers of al-Shaykhuniyya and al-Sarghatmishiyya, then it refers to silver, as in al-Mughrib: al-nuqra is a melted piece of gold or silver, and it is said 'nuqrat fidda' with addition for clarification. And in al-Misbah: al-nuqra is a melted piece of silver, and before melting it is 'tibr'.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on interpreting ambiguous price specifications in sales. When a price is stated as 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver', it is divided equally because the term mithqal is applied equally to both, so half is gold and half is silver. However, if the phrase is 'a thousand from gold and silver' (without mithqal), then the silver portion is in dirhams (coins) of standard weight, not mithqals. The commentary then discusses how the term 'dirham' varies by custom: in some regions it means a standard silver coin of 7 mithqals, in others like Egypt it means copper coins unless specified as silver. This applies to all transactions including marriage dower (mahr), bequests (wasiyya), leases (ijara), etc. The author also mentions that if a waqif in Egypt specifies dirhams without restriction, it defaults to copper fulus, but if he says 'nuqra' (melted silver), it refers to silver.

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqāl): A unit of weight for gold and silver; in this context equal to the standard weight of a dinar or dirham.
الدراهم (al-darāhim): Silver coins; their weight and value are determined by local custom.
النقرة (al-nuqra): Melted metal, specifically silver or gold; in waqf terms, indicates pure silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells an item for 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver', how much gold is due?
A) One thousand mithqals of gold
B) Five hundred mithqals of gold and five hundred of silver
C) The amount customarily given at the time of contract
D) One thousand mithqals of gold and one thousand of silver

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous price terms with equal attribution, the default is equal division, but custom can override in specific contexts like dirhams.

Paying Debt with Counterfeit Coins: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on discharging debt with counterfeit coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on discharging a debt of good coins with counterfeit coins and explain the practical difference between the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Paying a debt with counterfeit coins discharges it if the coins are lost, but Abu Yusuf protects the creditor's right to quality by allowing return of equivalent counterfeit.

When a debtor owes high-quality coins but pays with debased ones, does the debt get discharged? The Hanafi school has an internal division: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) emphasize that the coins are of the same genus, so use and loss end the obligation. Abu Yusuf, however, gives weight to the quality aspect, allowing the creditor to reclaim if he was unaware. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the majority position
Identify that the debtor owes good dirhams (jayyid) and pays counterfeit (zayf). Per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if the counterfeit coins are lost, the debt is discharged (qada) and the creditor has no recourse.
The ruling is absolute; it applies whether the creditor knew of the counterfeitness or not.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf holds that if the creditor was unaware, he can return an equivalent amount of counterfeit coins and demand the original good ones back, because his right includes quality like quantity.
This is based on the inability to get the good quality back.
3
Examine the proofs
The majority argue that counterfeit coins are of the same genus as good coins, proven by their validity in sarf (currency exchange) and salam. Therefore, quality has no independent value. Abu Yusuf reasons that the creditor's right to quality is frustrateable, so he can rescind the payment.
The majority's dalil is qiyas; Abu Yusuf's is istihsan.
4
Determine the practical difference and tarjih
The practical difference: under the majority, the debtor is free; under Abu Yusuf, the creditor can reclaim. The text indicates preference for Abu Yusuf's view as istihsan, citing Fakhr al-Islam.
Note that if the coins are still extant, the creditor can simply return them; the dispute only arises upon loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قضى زيف عن جيد وتلف فهو قضاء) يعني إذا كان له على آخر دراهم جياد فدفع له زيوفاً فهلكت كان قضاء وبرئ ولا رجوع عليه بشيء. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا علم بكونها زيوفاً أما إذا لم يعلم وإنما قيد بالتلف ليعلم حكم ما إذا أنفقها بالأولى وهذا عندهما. وقال أبو يوسف : إذا لم يعلم برد مثل زيوفه ويرجع بالجياد لأن حقه في الوصف كالقدر وقد تعذر الرجوع بصفة الجودة فتعين رد مثل المقبوض والرجوع بالجياد. ولهما أن المقبوض من جنس حقه بدليل أنه لو تجوز بها في الصرف والسلم الجاز، ولو لم يكن من الجنس لكان استبدالاً وهو حرام فلم يبق إلا الجودة ولا قيمة لها وقد حصل الاستيفاء، وذكر فخر الإسلام وغيره أن قولهما قياس، وقول أبي يوسف هو الاستحسان فظاهره ترجيح قول أبي يوسف. قيد بتلفها لأنها لو كانت قائمة ردها وفي الجوهرة من كتاب الرهن : إذا علم قبل أن ينفقها فطالبه بالجياد وأخذها كان الجياد أمانة في يده ما لم يرد الزيوف ويجدد القبض ا هـ. وفي الذخيرة : لو كان له عليه جياد فقضاه زيوفاً وقال أنفقها فإن لم ترج فردها علي ففعل فلم ترج فله أن يردها استحساناً...
His saying: (And if he paid counterfeit [coins] for good ones and they perished, it is payment) meaning if someone owed good dirhams to another and paid him counterfeit ones, and they perished, it is considered payment and he is absolved, and there is no recourse against him for anything. He made it absolute, so it includes whether he knew they were counterfeit or not; but he specified 'perished' to indicate the ruling for when he spends them a fortiori. This is according to them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]. And Abu Yusuf said: If he did not know, he returns the like of his counterfeit and claims back the good ones, because his right is in the description like the quantity, and recourse to the good quality has become impossible, so returning the equivalent of what was taken and claiming back the good ones became determined. And for them [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad], what was taken is of the same genus as his right, as evidenced by the fact that if he transacted with them in currency exchange or salam, it would be valid; and if it were not of the same genus, it would be a substitution which is prohibited. So nothing remains except the quality, and quality has no value, and satisfaction has been achieved. And Fakhr al-Islam and others mentioned that their saying is qiyas, and the saying of Abu Yusuf is istihsan, so apparently [this] indicates preference for Abu Yusuf's saying. It is restricted by their perishing because if they were still in existence, he would return them. And in al-Jawhara from the Book of Rahn: If he knew before spending them, and demanded the good ones and took them, the good ones are a trust in his hand until he returns the counterfeit and renews the taking. And in al-Dhakhira: If someone owed him good dirhams, and he paid him counterfeit ones and said, 'Spend them, and if they are not accepted, return them to me,' and he did so, and they were not accepted, then he may return them by istihsan...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a debtor owes good-quality dirhams (sound coins) but instead pays counterfeit (debased) coins. If the creditor accepts them and then the counterfeit coins are lost or spent, the debt is discharged according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two). Their reasoning is that the coins are of the same genus (silver), just of lower quality, and since quality has no independent value in exchange, the debt is satisfied. Abu Yusuf disagrees: if the creditor was unaware of the counterfeitness, he can return an equivalent amount of counterfeit coins and reclaim his good ones, because the right to quality is like the right to quantity, and since the quality is lost, he must return what he received and demand the original. The commentator notes that the two's view is considered qiyas (analogy) and Abu Yusuf's istihsan (juristic preference), implying preference for Abu Yusuf. The ruling is restricted to the case where the counterfeit coins perished; if they still exist, the creditor can simply return them and demand good ones.

Key Terms
زيف (zayf): Counterfeit or debased coins; coins of inferior quality but same metallic composition.
جيد (jayyid): Good quality coins; full-weight sound silver dirhams.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Payment of a debt; in this context, the legal discharge of obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the status of a debt of good dirhams if the debtor pays counterfeit dirhams and the creditor loses them?
A) The debt is not discharged; the debtor must pay again
B) The debt is discharged; the creditor cannot claim anything more
C) The creditor can return the loss to the debtor and demand good coins
D) The debtor must pay the difference in value

Key Takeaway
Paying a debt with counterfeit coins discharges the debt upon loss according to the majority, but Abu Yusuf's istihsan allows the creditor to reclaim if he was unaware, reflecting the tension between genus and quality in debt repayment.

Paying Bad Coins for Good Coin Debt: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on bad coin settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on paying bad coins for a good coin debt when the coins are lost, and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Bad coins of the same genus as the debt discharge it, but if the debtor is unaware, Abu Yusuf requires compensation.

We have learned the basic rule on discharging debts with currency. Now we examine a common scenario: the debtor pays with counterfeit or defective coins. The ruling hinges on whether the bad coins are of the same genus (jins) as the debt. In the Hanafi school, there is a well-known difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two) and Imam Abu Yusuf. This lesson will dissect their proofs and identify the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and default ruling
Recognize the facts: a debtor owes good dirhams and pays bad dirhams (ziyūf). The creditor accepts and later loses them. The default position (Zahir al-Riwayah from the two imams) is that it is a valid settlement; no further action.
This applies whether or not the debtor knew the coins were bad; the text says 'absolute'.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf disagrees when the debtor was unaware of the defect. He argues that the right includes the quality (jūdah) as well as quantity. Since quality cannot be returned, the debtor must return the like of the bad coins and can reclaim the good ones.
This is a key exception based on the debtor's knowledge.
3
Understand the proof structure
The two imams use qiyas (analogy): bad coins are of the same genus because they can be used in sarf (currency exchange) and salam; thus genus is preserved. Quality has no independent value, so the debt is discharged. Abu Yusuf uses istihsan (juristic preference): the right includes quality, so compensation is necessary.
Fakhral-Islam identifies the two's view as qiyas and Abu Yusuf's as istihsan.
4
Identify the tarjih (preference)
Later scholars (e.g., Fakhral-Islam in Al-Hidayah) indicate that when the debtor is unaware, Abu Yusuf's view is preferred. The mufta bihi in that specific circumstance is Abu Yusuf's opinion.
In other circumstances (debtor aware), the two's view is undisputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قضى زيف عن جيد وتلف فهو قضاء) يعني إذا كان له على آخر دراهم جياد فدفع له زيوفاً فهلكت كان قضاء وبرئ ولا رجوع عليه بشيء. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا علم بكونها زيوفاً أما إذا لم يعلم وإنما قيد بالتلف ليعلم حكم ما إذا أنفقها بالأولى وهذا عندهما. وقال أبو يوسف : إذا لم يعلم برد مثل زيوفه ويرجع بالجياد لأن حقه في الوصف كالقدر وقد تعذر الرجوع بصفة الجودة فتعين رد مثل المقبوض والرجوع بالجياد. ولهما أن المقبوض من جنس حقه بدليل أنه لو تجوز بها في الصرف والسلم الجاز، ولو لم يكن من الجنس لكان استبدالاً وهو حرام فلم يبق إلا الجودة ولا قيمة لها وقد حصل الاستيفاء، وذكر فخر الإسلام وغيره أن قولهما قياس، وقول أبي يوسف هو الاستحسان فظاهره ترجيح قول أبي يوسف. قيد بتلفها لأنها لو كانت قائمة ردها وفي الجوهرة من كتاب الرهن : إذا علم قبل أن ينفقها فطالبه بالجياد وأخذها كان الجياد أمانة في يده ما لم يرد الزيوف ويجدد القبض ا هـ. وفي الذخيرة : لو كان له عليه جياد فقضاه زيوفاً وقال أنفقها فإن لم ترج فردها علي ففعل فلم ترج فله أن يردها استحساناً. هذا فرق بين وبين ما إذا اشترى عيناً فوجد بها عيباً فأراد ردها فقال له البائع بعه فإن لم يشتره أحد فرده علي فعرضه على البيع فلم يشتره أحد ليس له أن منه يرده. والفرق أن المقبوض من الدراهم ليس عين حق القابض بل هو من جنس حقه لو تجوز به جاز وصار عين حقه، فإذا لم يتجوز بقي على ملك الدافع فصح أمر الدافع بالتصرف فيه فهو في الابتداء تصرف للدافع وفي الانتهاء لنفسه بخلاف التصرف في العين لأنها ملكه فتصرفه لنفسه فبطل خياره ا.هـ. وقدمنا أن الزيوف كالجياد في خمس مسائل كما في الولوالجية، وزدنا في أول كتاب البيوع سادساً عند الكلام على الأثمان. قيدنا الخلاف بعدم الأجرة. العلم لأنه لو علم بها وأنفقها كان قضاء اتفاقاً وقيد بالزيوف لأنها لو كانت ستوقة أو نبهرجة فأتلفها فإنه يرد مثلها ويرجع بالجياد اتفاقاً وهما فرقا بأن الزيوف من جنس حقه والستوقة والنبهرجة لا . وفي المصباح : زافت الدراهم تزيف زيفاً من باب «سار» ردأت ثم وصف بالمصدر فقيل درهم زيف مثل فلس وفلوس وربما قيل زائف على الأصل ودراهم زيف مثل راكع وركع، وزيفتها تزييفاً أظهرت زيفها . قال بعضهم: الدراهم الزيوف هي المطلية بالزئبق المعقود بمزاوجة الكبريت وكانت معروفة قبل زمانا وقدرها مثل سنج الميزان وفي الواقعات الحسامية من البيع تكلموا في معرفة الزيوف والنبهرجة. قال أبو النصر : الزيوف دراهم مغشوشة، أما النبهرجة التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان والستوقة صفر مموه بالفضة. وقال الفقيه أبو جعفر : الزيوف ما زيفه بيت المال يقال في عرفنا غطريقي لا غير والنبهرجة ما لا يقبله التاجر ا هـ.
His saying: (And if he paid bad coin for good coin and it was lost, then it is a settlement) means: If someone owes another good dirhams and pays him bad dirhams, and they are destroyed, it is a settlement and he is discharged, and there is no recourse against him for anything. He made it absolute, so it includes when he knows they are bad; as for if he does not know, he restricted it to being lost to make known the ruling of when he spends them, by way of priority, and this is according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: If he did not know, he must return the like of his bad coins and reclaim the good ones, because his right is in the description as well as the quantity, and since returning with the quality of goodness is impossible, returning the like of what was taken and reclaiming the good ones is determined. For them (the two), what was taken is of the same genus as his right, evidenced by the fact that if he were to transact with them in sarf or salam, it would be permissible. If it were not of the same genus, it would be an exchange, which is forbidden. Nothing remains except quality, and it has no value, and the debt has been received. Fakhral-Islam and others mentioned that their statement is qiyas, and Abu Yusuf's statement is istihsan, so apparently it indicates a preference for Abu Yusuf's statement. He restricted it to loss because if they were still in existence, he would return them. In al-Jawhara from the book of Rahn: If he knows before spending them, then demands the good ones and takes them, the good ones remain an amana in his hand until he returns the bad ones and renews the qabd. In al-Dhakhira: If someone has good dirhams owed to him, and the debtor pays him bad dirhams and says, 'Spend them, and if they don't circulate, return them to me,' and he does so, and they don't circulate, then he (the creditor) has the right to return them by istihsan. This is a distinction between this and the case of buying a specific item and finding a defect, wanting to return it, when the seller says 'Sell it, if no one buys it, return it to me,' and he offers it for sale and no one buys it: he does not have the right to return it. The difference is that what was taken from the dirhams is not the specific object of the taker's right, but rather of the same genus as his right; if he transacts with it, it is permissible and becomes his specific right. If he does not transact, it remains the property of the giver, so the giver's command to dispose of it is valid. Thus, initially it is a disposal for the giver, and ultimately for himself, contrary to disposal of a specific item because it is his property, so his disposal is for himself, thus his option is void. We have presented that bad coins are like good coins in five issues, as in al-Walwalijiyya, and we added a sixth at the beginning of the book of sales when discussing currencies. We restricted the disagreement to the absence of wage. Knowledge because if he knew and spent them, it is a settlement by agreement. He restricted to bad coins because if they were nabarahaj or sustainira and he destroyed them, then he must return their like and reclaim the good ones by agreement. Both (the two) distinguished that bad coins are of the same genus as his right, whereas sustainira and nabarahaj are not. In al-Misbah: The dirhams become bad (zafat) with zayf, from the pattern 'sara', meaning to become defective; then it is described by the verbal noun, so it is said 'dirham zayf' like 'falas' and 'fulus', and sometimes 'za'if' is said on the original pattern, and 'darahim zayf' like 'raki' and 'ruka'; and 'zayyaf'taha tazyifan' means I showed its badness. Some said: The bad dirhams are those coated with mercury bound with sulfur, and they were known before our time, and their weight is like the scale weight. In al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya from the book of Sale, they discussed knowing bad coins and nabarahaj. Abu al-Nasr said: Bad coins are counterfeit dirhams; as for nabarahaj, they are those struck outside the sultan's mint; and sustainira is brass plated with silver. Faqih Abu Ja'far said: Bad coins are those rejected by the treasury; in our custom it is called 'ghatriqi' only, and nabarahaj is that which the merchant does not accept.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling when a debtor pays bad coins (زيوف) for a debt of good coins (جياد) and the bad coins are lost. The default position, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, is that it is a valid settlement and the debtor has no further liability, regardless of whether he knew the coins were bad. The reason is that the bad coins are of the same genus (جنس) as the debt, and quality (جودة) has no independent monetary value; thus the debt is discharged upon loss. Abu Yusuf disagrees: if the debtor was unaware of the defect, he must return the like of the bad coins and can reclaim the good ones. For him, the right includes the quality (وصف الجودة), and since it is impossible to return the quality, the like of what was taken must be returned. The majority (the two) argue that if the bad coins were not of the same genus, using them in a currency exchange (صرف) or salam would be invalid, but it is not; therefore they are of the same genus. Fakhral-Islam (al-Marghinani) notes that the position of the two is based on qiyas (analogy), while Abu Yusuf's is istihsan (juristic preference), and he implies a preference for Abu Yusuf's view. The text further distinguishes between cases where the coins are lost versus still in hand, and between bad coins and other types like sustainira (ستوقة) or nabarahaj (نبهرجة), which are not of the same genus and always require return of the like. The passage also includes dictionary definitions of these terms from al-Misbah and al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in later practice leans toward Abu Yusuf's view when the payer is unaware, though the earlier authoritative transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) records the view of the two as the standard.

Key Terms
زيوف (ziyūf): Bad counterfeit dirhams that are of the same genus as genuine dirhams but defective in quality; often plated or mixed with base metal.
جياد (jiyād): Good quality dirhams of full weight and purity.
ستوقة (sutūqah): Coins made of brass plated with silver (base metal with silver coating).
نبهرجة (nabaharjah): Coins struck outside the official mint (illicit coinage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a debtor pays bad coins to a creditor who is unaware, and the bad coins are lost, what is the ruling?
A) The debtor must return the like of the bad coins and reclaim the good ones.
B) The debt is discharged and the debtor has no further obligation.
C) The creditor may deduct the value of the defect from the debt.
D) The debtor must pay the full value of the good coins.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, paying bad coins of the same genus discharges the debt; but if the debtor is unaware, the preferred position (Abu Yusuf) requires compensation.

Custom and Intent in Interpreting 'Dirham' in Waqf Conditions
Explains interpretation of 'dirham' in waqf conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on interpreting the term 'dirham' in waqf conditions according to Egyptian custom and apply the principles of urf and intent of the endower.

Core Idea
Unqualified 'dirham' in Egyptian waqf conditions means copper coins unless intent or qualification specifies silver.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author addresses a practical problem: what does 'dirham' mean in a waqf condition? In Egypt, the customary usage (urf) of the word differs from its literal meaning. The Hanafi school balances the established custom with the presumed intent of the endower, and also considers the classical default that 'dirham' refers to silver. Understanding this balance is crucial for correctly enforcing waqf conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the regional custom
In Egypt, the unqualified term 'dirham' refers to copper coins (fulus) of a specific weight, while in Syria and Hijaz it refers to a different weight. Recognize that custom (urf) can override the literal meaning.
Custom is a source of Islamic law when not contradicting primary texts.
2
Note the effect of qualification
When 'dirham' is qualified with 'silver' (فضة) or 'nuqrah' (نقرة), it reverts to meaning a silver dirham. 'Nuqrah' specifically indicates a piece of melted silver.
The term 'nuqrah' in waqf deeds like al-Shaykhuniyya and al-Sarghatmishiyya signals silver.
3
Apply the rule to waqf conditions
If an endower in Egypt stipulates 'dirham' without qualification, it is interpreted as copper coins. If qualified with 'nuqrah', it is silver. If the endower's intent is known, that prevails; if unknown, default to silver (the original meaning).
Al-Nahr critiques a blanket application of the Egyptian custom, arguing it depends on the time and intent.
4
Consider the Maliki fatwa and Hanafi preference
A Maliki scholar (al-Laqqani) fatwa that the dirham equals half and three copper coins. However, the preferred Hanafi view is a medium dirham, as per Jami' al-Fusulayn, because the principle is to take the certain (lowest) and avoid doubt.
The final relied-upon value in endowments like al-Shaykhuniyya is 'half and half' (i.e., a medium dirham).

Source Text
